Denying Human Independence Contradicts Prophethood
Argument that denying human agency contradicts prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying that humans are independent in their actions undermines the very mission of the prophets.

Core Idea
Human capacity for action is essential to prophethood.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a powerful argument that any theological position which strips humans of causal efficacy in their actions directly contradicts the message of the prophets. The author grounds this in the undeniable fact that prophets issued commands and prohibitions, which presuppose human ability to choose and act. As we explore the text, we'll see how this argument serves as a crucial link between the doctrines of qadar (divine decree) and nubuwwa (prophethood).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally before you analyse it.
The passage is dense; reading it twice helps.
2
Identify the key premises
Notice the opening statement that people are capable of good and evil, and the reference to the mutawatir tradition. These are the factual grounds.
3
Follow the conditional argument
Trace the 'if…then…' structure: if the servant is not independent, then they are prevented from actions, which contradicts the prophets. See how each step builds on the previous.
4
Connect to the conclusion about prophethood
The conclusion is that denying human origination of actions is incompatible with affirming prophethood. Ask yourself why this connection is necessary.
This is the core teaching point of the lesson.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument affects your understanding of free will and divine decree. Does it resolve or complicate the issue?
The passage does not fully resolve the problem, but it shows a necessary link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخيرات والشرور متمكنون فيهما غير ممنوعين عنهما ، فمن شاء فليؤمن ، ومن شاء فليكفر ، وثبت بالمتواتر المقطوع به أنهم كانوا يمدحون من آمن وأطاع ، ويذمون من كفر وعصى . فلو قلنا : العبد غير مستقل بالفعل ، كان ذلك تصريحاً بكونهم ممنوعين من الأفعال، غير قادرين عليها . وذلك تصريح بتكذيب الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - في عين تلك الأشياء ، التي ادعوا كونهم رسل الله فيها . والتصريح بمثل هذا التكذيب ، ينافي الاعتراف بكونهم صادقين . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن القول بأن العبد غير موجد لأفعال نفسه ، ينافي الإقرار بالنبوة .
People are capable of good and evil, not prevented from them; so whoever wills let him believe, and whoever wills let him disbelieve. And it is established by the absolutely continuous (mutawatir) tradition that they used to praise whoever believed and obeyed, and blame whoever disbelieved and disobeyed. If we say: the servant is not independent in action, that would be an explicit statement that they are prevented from actions, not able to perform them. And that is an explicit contradiction of the prophets—peace be upon them—regarding the very things for which they claimed to be messengers of God. And an explicit statement of such contradiction is incompatible with acknowledging that they are truthful. So it is established by these three aspects that the statement that the servant is not the originator (mujid) of his own actions is incompatible with affirming prophethood.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that denying human causal efficacy in actions contradicts the recognition of prophethood. The author begins by stating a premise: people are capable of good and evil, not prevented from them. This is supported by the Qur'anic verse 'Whoever wills, let him believe; whoever wills, let him disbelieve' and by the mutawatir tradition that the prophets praised those who obeyed and blamed those who disobeyed. The argument then proceeds: if we say that the servant is not independent (mustaqill) in his actions, then that implies he is prevented from actions and unable to perform them. But that would be a denial of the very things for which the prophets claimed to be messengers—since the prophets commanded actions and forbade others, implying human capacity. Such a denial contradicts the acknowledgment that the prophets are truthful. Therefore, the conclusion is that denying that the servant is the originator (mujid) of his own actions is incompatible with affirming prophethood. The phrase 'by these three aspects' refers to earlier points not fully quoted in this window, but the overall argument is clear: prophethood entails human capacity to act, so any denial of that capacity undermines prophethood.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage does not deny divine power; it shows that human capacity is a necessary condition for prophethood. The argument is about logical consistency, not about the extent of divine control.

Why Miracles Only Prove Prophethood on the Ash'ari View
First rebuttal: miracle proof relies on Ash'ari denial of human creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rebuttal that the Mu'tazili view on human power undermines the certainty of miracles, while the Ash'ari view secures it.

Core Idea
If you can't be sure who caused the miracle, you can't be sure of prophethood.

This passage presents the first of three rebuttals against the objection that Ash'ari denial of human causal efficacy destroys the proof of miracles. The author turns the tables: he argues that it is actually the Mu'tazili view that makes miracles uncertain, because if a human can create, then a miracle could be from the prophet himself. Only on the Ash'ari view is the miracle a certain sign from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: claim, reason, contrast, conclusion.
The passage uses the phrase 'فلما' to indicate contrast.
2
Identify the core claim
The author's main point is that the objection applies to the Mu'tazila themselves, not the Ash'aris. Find how he states this.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why does the Mu'tazili view on human power (qudra) create uncertainty? Because if humans can originate actions, then a miracle could be from the prophet. The Ash'ari view that humans cannot create removes this doubt.
4
Follow the contrast
Notice the contrast between the Mu'tazila (who admit human power can create) and the Ash'aris (who deny it). See how the author dismisses the Mu'tazila's own proof that only God can create bodies.
5
Formulate the conclusion
The passage ends with a clear conclusion: the miracle indicates truthfulness only on our (Ash'ari) doctrine. Make sure you see why.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن المعتمد في الجواب عن هذا المقام ، أن نقول : هذا الذي الزمتموه علينا ، لازم عليكم من وجوه : الأول : إنكم لما اعترفتم بأن قدرة العبد صالحة للإيجاد . لم يمكنكم القطع بأن فاعل هذه المعجزات هو الله تعالى . ونحن قد حكينا في باب دلائلنا العقلية : دليل المعتزلة على أن غير الله تعالى، لا يصح منه خلق الجسم والحياة . وبينا ضعفه وسقوطه . وأما نحن فلما قلنا : إن قدرة العبد لا تصلح للإيجاد ، لم يتوجه هذا السؤال علينا البتة . فثبت : أن دلالة المعجز على صدق المدعي ، إنما تتم على مذهبنا ، لا على مذهبكم .
Know that the relied-upon response to this topic is that we say: This which you have required of us is also required of you in several aspects. The first: Since you acknowledge that the power of the servant is capable of origination (ijad), you cannot be certain that the agent of these miracles is God Almighty. And we have related in the chapter of our rational proofs the proof of the Mu'tazila that none other than God can create a body or life, and we have shown its weakness and invalidity. As for us, since we said that the power of the servant is not fit for origination, this question does not arise against us at all. So it is established that the indication of the miracle (mu'jiz) of the truthfulness of the claimant is only complete on our doctrine, not on your doctrine.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is the first of three rebuttals to the objection that the Ash'ari denial of human causal efficacy undermines prophethood. The author begins by stating that the objection they (the Mu'tazila) have made against the Ash'aris actually applies to the Mu'tazila themselves. The first aspect: The Mu'tazila claim that human power (qudra) is capable of origination (ijad) — that is, it can create effects. If so, then when a miracle occurs at the hands of a prophet, one cannot be certain that it is from God alone, because the miracle could potentially be the product of the prophet's own power. The Mu'tazila have their own rational proof that only God can create bodies and lives, but the author says they have already shown that proof to be weak and invalid. In contrast, the Ash'aris hold that human power cannot create anything; it only has the capacity to act in a way that is not truly creative. Therefore, since human power cannot produce miracles, the miracle must be from God, and that establishes the truthfulness of the claimant. Thus, only on the Ash'ari doctrine is the miracle a certain proof of prophethood. This rebuttal turns the tables on the Mu'tazila: their own view undermines the evidential value of miracles.

Key Terms
قدرة العبد (power of the servant)
الإيجاد (origination/creation)
المعجز (miracle)
الدلالة (indication/proof)
المدعي (claimant of prophethood)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that miracles are proof of prophethood regardless of one's view on human power.
Correction: This passage shows that only if human power is incapable of creating can miracles be uniquely attributed to God and thus serve as certain proof.

Checkpoint
According to the first rebuttal, why does the Mu'tazili view on human power weaken the proof of miracles?
A) Because they deny miracles altogether.
B) Because if human power can create, a miracle could be from the prophet himself, so certainty is lost.
C) Because they require multiple witnesses for miracles.
D) Because they believe miracles are only apparent, not real.

Key Takeaway
The first rebuttal shows that only on the Ash'ari doctrine, where human power cannot create, does a miracle serve as a certain proof of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The certainty of prophethood in this passage hinges on a correct belief about human power. How certain are you that your beliefs about God's exclusive agency are not just intellectual but lived? What practical step can you take this week to make that belief real in your daily reliance on Him?

Third Rebuttal: Miracles for Liars Can Have Other Purposes
The third objection argues that a miracle for a liar is not necessarily evil if done for another purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God may create miracles for reasons other than confirming a prophet, and that this does not make them evil.

Core Idea
A miracle for a liar is only evil if God's purpose is to confirm him; God has many purposes.

In this passage, the author addresses the objection that a miracle occurring at the hand of a liar would be evil because it misleads. He argues that if God creates the miracle for a different purpose—like testing people—then it is not evil. He also refutes the idea that even the appearance of evil is wrong, using the example of ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow of the argument: a conditional statement, an objection, and a refutation.
Pay attention to the term 'قبيح' (evil) and how it is defined by purpose.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author contrasts two possibilities: the miracle created for the purpose of confirming the liar (evil) vs. for another purpose (not evil). Find where this distinction is made.
Look for the phrase 'إنما يقبح إذا كان غرض الله منه تصديق ذلك الكاذب'.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The objector says that even if for another purpose, the miracle gives an impression of evil. The author answers using the example of mutashabihat. Understand why that analogy works.
The key point: not every act that suggests evil is itself evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وهو أن إظهار المعجز على هذا الكاذب ، إنما يقبح إذا كان غرض الله منه : تصديق ذلك الكاذب . أما لو خلق الله المعجز عقيب دعوى الكاذب . لا لغرض التصديق ، بل لغرض آخر . لم يقبح البتة . ومن المعلوم : أن أغراض الله تعالى في خلق المخلوقات كثيرة ، غير مضبوطة . ولما ثبت أنه يجوز أن يخلق الله تعالى ذلك المعجز عقيب دعوى الكاذب ، لغرض آخر سوى التصديق ، وثبت أن على هذا التقدير لا يقبح خلقه ، علمنا : أنه لا يقبح من الله تعالى خلق المعجز ، عقيب دعوى الكاذب . ولا يقال : إنه يقبح من الله تعالى خلق المعجز عقيب دعوى الكاذب مطلقاً . وذلك لأنه تعالى إن خلقه لغرض تصديقه ، فهو قبيح . وإن خلقه لغرض آخر فهو أيضاً قبيح . لأنه يوهم كونه واقعاً لغرض التصديق . وكما أن فعل القبيح قبيح ، فكذا فعل ما يوهم القبيح قبيح أيضاً . لأنا نقول : لا نسلم أن فعل ما يوهم القبيح : قبيح . ألا ترى أن إنزال المتشابهات قبح المكلف تقصيراً من حيث قطع لا في موضع القطع . فكذا ههنا . لما ثبت أن خلق المعجز ، عقيب دعوى الكاذب ، يحتمل وجهاً آخر ، سوى التصديق .
The third: That the manifestation of a miracle at the hand of a liar is only evil if God's purpose from it is to confirm that liar. But if God creates the miracle after the liar's claim, not for the purpose of confirmation but for another purpose, it is not evil at all. And it is known that the purposes of God in creating creatures are many and unlimited. Since it is established that it is permissible for God to create that miracle after the liar's claim for another purpose besides confirmation, and it is established that on this assumption its creation is not evil, we know that it is not evil for God to create a miracle after a liar's claim. And it cannot be said: It is evil for God to create a miracle after a liar's claim absolutely. Because if He creates it for the purpose of confirming him, it is evil. And if He creates it for another purpose, it is also evil, because it gives the impression that it occurred for the purpose of confirmation. And just as performing an evil act is evil, so also doing something that suggests evil is also evil. We say: We do not concede that doing something that suggests evil is evil. Do you not see that sending down ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) is made evil for the responsible person as a shortcoming from the standpoint of certainty in a place where there is no certainty? So also here, since it is established that the creation of a miracle after a liar's claim may have another aspect besides confirmation.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is the third rebuttal, addressing the objection that it would be evil for God to create a miracle at the hand of a liar because it would mislead people. The author argues that such a miracle is only evil if God's purpose is to confirm the liar. But God may have other purposes, and His purposes are many and not limited. Therefore, it is permissible for God to create a miracle after a false claim for another reason, and that is not evil. Then the author anticipates an objection: one might say that even if it is for another purpose, it is still evil because the miracle gives the impression that God is confirming the liar. The author refutes this by saying that giving an impression of evil is not necessarily evil. The example given is that of ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in the Quran. These verses are sent down, and they may give an impression of something problematic, but they are not evil because there is a valid aspect. Similarly, a miracle after a liar's claim may have a valid aspect other than confirmation, so it is not evil. The argument thus defends the possibility that God could allow miracles to occur for apparent liars without that being morally problematic, because divine wisdom may have other considerations.

Key Terms
المعجز (miracle)
الكاذب (liar)
التصديق (confirmation)
الغرض (purpose)
المتشابهات (ambiguous verses)
القبيح (evil/ugly)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any miracle occurring after a liar's claim is evil regardless of God's purpose.
Correction: If God has another purpose besides confirmation, the miracle is not evil. Even the appearance of evil is not necessarily evil, as shown by ambiguous verses.

Checkpoint
According to the third rebuttal, why is it not necessarily evil for God to create a miracle after a liar's claim?
A) Because liars are also worthy of miracles.
B) Because God's purposes are many and the miracle may have another purpose besides confirming the liar.
C) Because miracles are only apparent, not real.
D) Because the liar will be punished anyway.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of miracles can serve multiple purposes, and an action is not evil if it is not intended for evil, even if it appears to be.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone who appears to be supported by signs but is not truthful, do you immediately assume God is confirming them? Do you consider that God may have other purposes you do not see? Will you train your heart to suspend judgment and trust in divine wisdom beyond appearances?
?
In your own life, when you face trials that seem meaningless or even evil, do you ever consider that God might have a purpose other than what you assume? Will you commit to seeking His wisdom rather than concluding that He has abandoned you?

Answer One: Islam as a Non-Enduring Accident
First answer: Islam as a non-enduring accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil is resolved by considering Islam as an accident that does not endure over time.

Core Idea
An accident needs constant recreation, so future request is not contradictory.

The objection against Abraham's prayer assumes that Islam is a permanent state. The first answer shifts the perspective: Islam is an accident that does not persist. By understanding this, the request for Islam in future time becomes a request for new creation, not repetition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the first answer.
Pay attention to the definition of Islam as an accident.
2
Identify the key concept
Notice that Islam is defined as an accident (ʿaraḍ). Why does that matter?
3
Connect to the objection
See how this concept resolves the problem of requesting what is already possessed.
4
Reflect
Think about how this applies to other aspects of faith that require continuous divine creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله: «لما كانا مسلمين، كان إقدامها على طلب الإسلام طلباً لتحصيل الحاصل. وهو محال»، فنقول: الجواب عنه من وجوه: الأول: إن الإسلام عرض قائم بالقلب، والعرض لا يبقى. فقوله: اجعلنا مسلمين لك، معناه: طلب أن يخلق الله ذلك العرض في قلبه في الزمان المستقبل. ومعلوم أن طلب تحصيله في الزمان المستقبل، لا ينافي كونه حاصلاً في الحال. الثاني: أن يكون المراد منه: الزيادة في الإسلام، كقوله تعالى: ليزدادوا إيماناً مع إيمانهم وقوله: والذين أهتدوا زادهم هدى، وقال إبراهيم عليه السلام: ولكن ليطمئن قلبي. الثالث: إن لفظ الإسلام إذا ذكر خالياً عن الصلات، أفاد الإيمان، أما إذا قرن بحرف اللام، كقوله: مسلمين لك، فالمراد الاستسلام والانقياد والرضا، بكل ما قضاه الله. فيحتمل: أن يكون المراد بقوله: اجعلنا مسلمين لك: ما ذكرناه. والله أعلم
As for his statement: 'Since they were Muslims, their undertaking to request Islam is a request for the acquisition of what is already possessed (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible,' we say: the answer to it has several aspects. First: Islam is an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in the heart, and an accident does not endure. So his saying 'Make us Muslims (muslimīna) for You' means a request that God create that accident in his heart in future time. It is known that requesting its acquisition in future time does not contradict its being present in the present moment. Second: It could mean increase in Islam, like His statement 'so that they may increase in faith upon their faith' and His statement 'and those who are guided, He increases them in guidance,' and Abraham said 'but so that my heart may be at ease.' Third: The word 'Islam' when mentioned without a connection (ṣila) indicates faith (īmān); but when connected with the letter lām, as in 'muslimīna laka,' the intended meaning is submission (istislām), compliance (inqiyād), and contentment (riḍā) with everything God has decreed. So it is possible that the intended meaning of His statement 'make us Muslims for You' is what we mentioned. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection raised against the prayer of Abraham (peace be upon him) and his son Ishmael: 'Our Lord, make us Muslims for You' (Quran 2:128). The objection claims that since they were already Muslims, asking for Islam is requesting something already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. The author provides three distinct answers. The first answer explains that Islam is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that does not endure over time; therefore, the prayer asks God to create that accident in future moments, which does not contradict its present existence. The second answer interprets the request as seeking increase in Islam, supported by Quranic verses about increased faith and guidance, and by Abraham's own statement about seeking reassurance for his heart. The third answer distinguishes between the word 'Islam' when used absolutely (indicating faith) and when used with the particle 'for You' (laka), which then denotes submission and compliance to God's decree. Thus the prayer may be requesting steadfastness in submission, not a repetition of something already possessed. Each answer resolves the apparent impossibility by showing that the request is for something new or different from what is currently possessed.

Key Terms
تحصيل الحاصل
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islam is a permanent state that cannot be increased or renewed.
Correction: Islam is an accident that requires continuous creation, so asking for it in future is valid.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, why is it not impossible for Abraham to ask for Islam while already Muslim?
A) Because Islam is a substance that can be increased.
B) Because Islam is an accident that does not endure, so future request is for new creation.
C) Because the word 'Islam' means something different when used with 'laka'.
D) Because the prayer was for his son only.

Key Takeaway
The first answer resolves the objection by showing that Islam is an accident requiring continuous creation, so a future request is not for the already possessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on your past acts of faith as a guarantee that you are still a true Muslim today? How will you actively seek the renewal of your Islam in this very moment?
?
If your faith is an accident that does not endure by itself, what does that imply about your daily need for Allah's sustenance? What concrete step will you take today to ask Him to 'Make you a Muslim' anew?
?
When you recite the prayer 'Make us Muslims' in your salah, do you realize you are asking for a fresh creation? How does that change the way you approach your next prayer?

Answer Two: Request for Increase in Islam
Second answer: interpreting the prayer as request for increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the prayer for Islam can be interpreted as a request for increase, supported by Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Faith can increase, so asking for increase is not impossible.

The second answer interprets the prayer not as requesting something already possessed, but as seeking an increase in the degree of Islam. This is supported by Quranic verses that speak of increase in faith and guidance. The lesson explores this concept.

Answer Three: The Meaning of 'Muslimīna Laka'
Third answer: semantic distinction with particle 'laka'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third answer, which distinguishes between absolute Islam and Islam with 'laka' meaning submission to God's decree.

Core Idea
With 'laka', Islam means total submission, which can be requested.

The third answer offers a semantic distinction. The word 'Islam' when used absolutely means faith, but when connected with the particle 'laka' (for You), it signifies a deeper submission and compliance with God's will. Thus, Abraham's prayer is for this specific quality, not for the general faith he already had.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third answer.
Focus on the linguistic distinction.
2
Understand the linguistic distinction
Notice the role of the particle 'laka' in changing the meaning.
3
Connect to the prayer
The prayer becomes a request for complete submission.
4
Reflect on the concept of submission
How does this answer resolve the objection?

The Seventh Proof: Prophetic Confessions of Divine Control Over Faith and Disbelief
Author cites prophetic confessions as proof that faith and disbelief are from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verses of the prophets serve as a proof that faith and disbelief are from Allah, and how the author counters alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
The prophets' own prayers show that only Allah gives faith and prevents disbelief.

The author presents a compelling argument from the Qur'an itself: the prophets, the best of creation, acknowledged that their faith and protection from sin came directly from Allah. This lesson examines their testimonies and the author's response to those who might reinterpret them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice how the author lists specific verses where prophets attribute their faith and avoidance of sin to Allah's direct action.
The verses are from Ibrahim, Yusuf, Musa, and Muhammad (peace be upon them all).
2
Identify the four prophetic examples
For each prophet, note what they ask for or acknowledge. Abraham asks to be kept from idolatry; Joseph asks to be protected from temptation; Moses declares that misguidance is from Allah; Muhammad is told that Allah strengthened him.
Each example shows reliance on Allah for guidance.
3
Understand the objection
The objection suggests these verses could be about divine favors (alṭāf) or about Allah's judgment and naming (ḥukm wa tasmiya). Think about what these alternative meanings imply.
The author says the answer covers all cases comprehensively (istiqṣā').
4
Reflect on the author's response
The author does not elaborate the response here but states it is comprehensive. Consider why a single answer can refute both interpretations.
This shows the strength of the evidence from the prophets' own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن الله تعالى أخبر عن أكابر الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - أنهم اعترفوا بأن الكفر والإيمان من الله . فقال حكـايـة عن إبراهيم - عليـه السلام - : رب اجنبني وبني أن نعبد الأصنام (۱) ) وقال حكاية عن يوسف - عليه السلام - : ( وإن لا تصرف عني كيدهن (۳) ) إلى قوله : ( فصرف عنه كيدهن (٣) ) وقال حكاية عن موسى - عليه السلام - : ( إن هي إلا فتنتك تضل بها من تشاء (٤) ) وقال لمحمد صلوات الله عليه وسلامه : ( ولولا أن ثبتتاك ، لقد كدت تركن إليهم شيئاً قليلا (٥)) وكل هذه النصوص دالة على أن الإيمان والكفر من الله . فإن قيل : هذا محمول على فعل الألطاف ، أو على الحكم والتسمية . قلنا : سق الجواب عن الكل ، على سبيل الاستقصاء (1).
The seventh proof: Allah Almighty informed about the greatest prophets - peace be upon them - that they acknowledged that disbelief and faith are from Allah. He said relating from Ibrahim - peace be upon him -: 'My Lord, keep me and my sons away from worshiping idols' (14:35). And He said relating from Yusuf - peace be upon him -: 'And if You do not avert from me their plot' (12:33) up to His saying: 'So He averted from him their plot' (12:34). And He said relating from Musa - peace be upon him -: 'It is nothing but Your trial by which You lead astray whom You will' (7:155). And He said to Muhammad, peace and blessings upon him: 'And if We had not strengthened you, you would have almost inclined to them a little' (17:74). All these texts indicate that faith and disbelief are from Allah. If it is said: This is interpreted as the action of divine favors (alṭāf), or as judgment and naming. We say: The answer applies to all, comprehensively (1).
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (al-ḥujja al-sābiʿa) for the claim that faith (īmān) and disbelief (kufr) are from Allah. The author cites several Qur'anic passages where the prophets themselves admit their reliance on Allah for guidance and protection from sin. Specifically: (1) Abraham asks Allah to keep him and his sons away from idol worship (14:35). (2) Joseph says 'If You do not avert from me their plot, I may incline to them' (12:33) and Allah averted it (12:34). (3) Moses says 'It is nothing but Your trial by which You lead astray whom You will' (7:155). (4) To Muhammad, Allah says 'If We had not strengthened you, you would have almost inclined to them a little' (17:74). The author argues that these texts all show that faith (avoidance of shirk and sin) and disbelief (inclination to sin) are attributed to Allah's action. Then an objection is raised: these verses could be interpreted as referring to divine 'favors' (alṭāf) that help the believer, or to Allah's judgment (ḥukm) and naming (tasmiya) of the action. The author dismisses this objection with a sweeping answer (sūq al-jawāb ʿan al-kull ʿalā sabīl al-istiqṣā'), implying that a comprehensive refutation is available but not elaborated here.

Key Terms
الحجة السابعة
الكفر
الإيمان
الألطاف
الحكم والتسمية
الاستقصاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prophets' prayers imply that humans have independent free will to choose faith or disbelief.
Correction: The verses show that the prophets attributed their avoidance of sin and their faith to Allah's direct action and protection, not to their own independent choice.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the seventh proof?
A) The prophets had no free will
B) The Qur'anic verses prove that both faith and disbelief are from Allah because the prophets attributed their avoidance of sin and their faith to Allah's action
C) The prophets were wrong to ask Allah for help
D) The Qur'anic verses are ambiguous and cannot be used as proof

Key Takeaway
The prophets' confessions in the Qur'an demonstrate that faith and disbelief are entirely from Allah, and this argument cannot be reduced to mere divine favors or judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the confessions of the prophets, do you genuinely sense that your own faith is a gift from Allah, or do you unconsciously take credit for your belief? How would your prayer life change if you truly believed that every moment of īmān is sustained by Him alone?
?
The prophets begged Allah to protect them from disbelief and sin. When was the last time you pleaded with Allah with the same desperation to keep you from kufr or a specific sin? If you haven't, what does that say about your awareness of your own weakness?
?
The author refutes the idea that these verses are just about 'favors' or 'labels'. Do you ever find yourself explaining away Allah's direct control over your faith as merely 'help' or 'guidance'? What would it mean for your humility and gratitude to fully accept that even your ability to believe is created by Allah moment by moment?

Divine Commands Imply Human Agency
The author argues that divine commands presuppose the servant's ability to act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine commands and prohibitions logically require that humans are genuine agents capable of performing or refraining from actions.

Core Idea
If God commands, the servant must be able to obey.

This lesson examines the second proof for human agency based on the very nature of divine commands. The prophets universally taught that God commands and forbids; such commands would be meaningless if the servant were not truly capable of acting. The author uses a powerful analogy to show that commanding an incapable person is considered foolish by rational people, so it cannot be attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from prophetic consensus to the reductio ad absurdum.
Pay attention to the analogy of commanding an ill person.
2
Understand the logical entailment
The author argues that a command implies the commanded person's ability. Why would a command be unreasonable if the person cannot act?
3
Reflect on the analogy of inanimate objects
The author extends the argument to an extreme: commanding inanimate objects is absurd. This strengthens the case that the servant must be the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الأنبياء عليهم السلام أجمعوا على أن الله أمر عباده ببعض الأشياء ، ونهاهم عن بعضها . ولو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لما صح ذلك . فكيف يعقل أن يقول الله للعبد : افعل الإيمان والصلوات والعبادات ، ولا تفعل الكفر والمعاصي. مع أن الفاعل لهذه الأفعال ، والتارك لها ليس هو العبد ؟ فإن أمر الغير بالفعل يتضمن الإخبار عن كون ذلك المأمور قادراً على الفعل ، حتى إنه لو لم يكن المأمور قادراً عليه لمرض أو لسبب آخر ، ثم أمره غيره به. فإن العقلاء يتعجبون منه وينسبونه إلى الحماقة والجنون . ويقولون : إنك تعلم أنه لا يقدر على ذلك الفعل ، فكيف تأمره به ؟ ولو صح هذا لصح أن يبعث الله رسولاً إلى الجمادات مع المعجزات ، والكتب والشرائع . لأجل أن يبلغ ذلك الرسول تلك الشرائع إلى تلك الجمادات . ثم إنه تعالى يخلق الحياة والعقل لتلك الجمادات ، ويعاقبها ، لأجل أنهم لم يمتثلوا أمر الرسول ، حال كونها جمادات . وذلك مما يعلم فساده ببديهة العقل . واعلم : أنا قد حكينا لهم استدلالهم بما تقرر من هذا الوجه ، على أن علم العبد بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه : علم ضروري . وذلك الوجه مغاير لهذا الوجه . لأنا في ذلك الوجه استدللنا بحصول العلم الضروري ، ويحسن المدح والذم على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكون العبد موجداً . والآن نستدل بحسن المدح والذم على كون العبد موجداً . فظهر الفرق . ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العبد وإن لم يكن موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، إلا أنه مكتسب لها ويكفي هذا القدر في حسن المدح والذم ؟ لأنا نقول : دخول فعل العبد في الوجود ، إما أن يكون من العبد ، وإما أن لا يكون منه .
The second proof: The prophets, peace be upon them, unanimously agreed that God commanded His servants with some things and forbade them from some things. And if the servant were not the one who brings his actions into existence, that would not be correct. So how can it be reasonable for God to say to the servant: 'Do faith, prayers, and acts of worship, and do not do disbelief and sins' while the doer of these actions and the abandoner of them is not the servant? For commanding another to do an action entails informing that the commanded person is able to do it, so much so that if the commanded person were not able to do it due to illness or another reason, and then another commanded him to do it, intelligent people would be amazed at him and attribute him to foolishness and madness, and say: 'You know that he is not able to do that action, so how do you command him to do it?' And if this were valid, it would be valid for God to send a messenger to inanimate objects with miracles, books, and laws, so that that messenger would convey those laws to those inanimate objects, and then God would create life and intellect for those inanimate objects and punish them because they did not comply with the messenger's command while being inanimate. And that is known to be invalid by the primary intellect. And know: we have related for them their argument by what has been established from this aspect, that the servant's knowledge of his being the one who brings his actions into existence is necessary knowledge. And that aspect is different from this aspect. For in that aspect we inferred by the attainment of necessary knowledge, and the goodness of praise and blame, that necessary knowledge is attained of the servant being the one who brings into existence. And now we infer by the goodness of praise and blame that the servant is the one who brings into existence. So the difference has become clear. And it cannot be said: why is it not permissible to say: the servant, even if he is not the one who brings his actions into existence, yet he acquires them, and this amount suffices for the goodness of praise and blame? Because we say: The occurrence of the servant's action into existence is either from the servant or not from him.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ḥujjah al-thāniyah) for human agency, based on divine commands. The author argues that the prophets unanimously taught that God commands and forbids, which entails that the servant is capable of performing or refraining from actions. If the servant were not the agent (mūjid), then such commands would be absurd, as commanding an incapable person is considered foolish. The author illustrates this by an analogy: commanding an inanimate object would be even more absurd, implying that the servant must be the doer. Then the author distinguishes this argument from a previous one based on necessary knowledge: the earlier argument used the fact of necessary knowledge to prove agency; this one uses the goodness (ḥusn) of praise and blame to prove agency, since praise and blame are only appropriate if the servant is the agent. Finally, an objection is raised: what about acquisition (kasb)? The author refutes it by pointing out that the existence of the action must either come from the servant or not; if not, the servant has no real role and praise/blame are meaningless.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
مأمور
قادر
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine predestination negates human responsibility, so commands are mere formalities.
Correction: The passage shows that divine commands logically require the servant to be the real agent; otherwise the commands would be absurd.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about commanding an incapable person?
A) It is permissible if God does it.
B) It is considered foolish by rational people.
C) It shows the commander's power.
D) It is a matter of faith.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands logically imply that the servant is the real agent of his actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe you had the ability to obey? If so, what does your disobedience say about how you value God's command?
?
The author says commanding an incapable person is considered 'foolishness and madness'. Have you ever excused your own sin by telling yourself you were powerless? How does this passage dismantle that excuse?
?
If God's command proves you are the agent of your actions, why do you sometimes live as if you are a victim of your circumstances? What would change in your life today if you fully embraced that you are the one who chooses?

Distinguishing Proofs and Refuting Kasb
The author distinguishes his proof from the earlier one and refutes the acquisition theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the proof from necessary knowledge and the proof from praise/blame, and why the theory of kasb fails to preserve human agency.

Core Idea
Praise and blame are meaningless without genuine agency.

In this continuation, the author clarifies how the current argument differs from an earlier one. The earlier argument used the fact of necessary knowledge to prove agency; this one uses the goodness of praise and blame. Then he addresses an objection: what if the servant merely acquires (kasb) the action rather than creating it? The author shows that this distinction collapses because the action must originate from the servant or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the distinction and the kasb objection
Read both the Arabic and translation. Note the two arguments being compared and the kasb theory.
Look for the phrase 'فهذا الوجه مغاير' to see the distinction.
2
Understand the difference between the two arguments
Earlier argument: necessary knowledge of agency is known. Current argument: goodness of praise/blame directly proves agency. See how they are distinct.
3
Analyze the refutation of kasb
The author presents a dichotomy: either the servant brings the action into existence or not. Kasb is not a middle ground; if the action is not from the servant, praise/blame are unjustified.

The Fourth Argument: Human Agency and Divine Proofs
States that denying human origination of actions invalidates proofs of God and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying human origination of actions would undermine the proofs for God's existence, prophethood, and divine scriptures.

Core Idea
Without human origination, proofs for God and prophets collapse.

This lesson presents the fourth argument from the author's series of proofs. It draws a direct logical link between human agency and the very possibility of establishing religious truths. The argument is concise but profound: without human origination of actions, the entire edifice of proof for God, prophets, and revealed books collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's exact wording.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if not... then nullified'.
2
Identify the condition and consequence
Locate the condition (servant not being originator) and the consequence (nullification of proofs for God, prophethood, and scriptures).
This logical link is the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on why this connection is necessary
Consider why the author believes human agency is essential for these proofs. The commentary will help explain the reasoning.
Think about what it means for an action to be 'proof' of something.
4
Connect to earlier discussions
The author notes this chapter has been elaborated previously. If you have access to earlier sections, review them for deeper context.
This argument builds on earlier foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه لبطل القول بإثبات ، وبإثبات النبوة . وكون كتب الله المنزلة : حجة . وقد تقدم تقرير هذا الفصل .
The fourth proof: if the servant were not the originator of his own actions, the assertion of establishing [the existence of God] and establishing prophethood would be nullified, and that the revealed books of God are proof. And the elaboration of this chapter has preceded.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which is stated very concisely. It argues that if human beings do not originate their own actions, then all proofs for the existence of God, prophethood, and the authority of revealed scriptures would be invalid. The author says that the detailed discussion of this argument has already been given earlier (perhaps in a previous chapter).

The Fourth Proof: Human Agency and Prophethood
The author argues that denying human creation of actions invalidates prophethood and revealed books.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human creation of actions is necessary for the validity of prophethood and revealed scriptures.

Core Idea
Without human agency, prophethood and scripture lose their force as proofs.

This lesson examines a concise but powerful argument from the author. He contends that if humans do not originate their own actions, then the very concept of proof—and specifically the proof of prophethood and divine books—collapses. This argument links human responsibility to the meaningfulness of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the concise conditional structure.
The author uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the conditional statement and its conclusion
Find the 'if' clause (servant not originator) and the two consequences: invalidation of proof in general and invalidation of prophethood and scriptures as proof.
The word 'بطل' indicates nullification.
3
Reflect on why prophethood and scripture depend on human agency
Ponder: if humans do not truly act, can they be addressed by prophets? Are commandments meaningful? The author implies that prophethood and scriptures are proofs only if humans are responsible agents.
This connects human action to the purpose of revelation.
4
Connect this argument to the broader discussion of qadar
Recall that the author has explained this point earlier. This proof fits into the larger debate on divine predestination and human free will.

The Eighth Proof: The Absurdity of God Sending a Prophet for Evil
The author presents a reductio argument against God creating evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reductio ad absurdum argument used to prove that God does not create evil acts.

Core Idea
If God creates evil, He could send prophets for evil – an absurd conclusion.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful logical argument against the idea that God creates evil. By showing that such a premise leads to the absurdity of God sending a prophet to teach theft, Satan worship, and other evils, the author demonstrates that divine creation of evil is rationally impossible. This argument relies on the necessary connection between God's attributes and the content of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the conditional 'if... then...' structure.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic, but contains a full argument.
2
Identify the premise and its implications
The author starts with a conditional: if it is permissible for God to create evil acts (adultery, sodomy), then it would be permissible for Him to send a prophet with that as his religion. Identify this as the premise being attacked.
3
Trace the extension to absurd examples
The author extends the logic: if that were permissible, then among past prophets there could have been one sent only to call to theft, praising Satan, etc. Notice the escalation of absurdity.
These are all things that a true prophet would never do.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The implication is that since such a prophet is impossible (God only sends prophets with truth), the original premise (that God creates evil) must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
5
Connect to larger theological context
This proof links the attribute of divine justice and wisdom to the necessity of prophethood being only for good. Reflect on how this argument defends God's perfection.
It also shows the coherence of God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : لو جاز أن يخلق الزنا واللواطة ، لجاز أن يبعث رسولاً هذا دينه . ولو جاز ذلك ، لجاز فيما سلف من الأنبياء من لم يبعث إلا للدعوة إلى السرقة ، والأفعال القبيحة ، ومدح الشيطان وعبادته ، والاستخفاف بالله وبرسوله وبالوالدين ، وذم المحسن ومدح المسيء
The Eighth Proof: If it were permissible for Him to create adultery (الزنا) and sodomy (اللواطة), then it would be permissible for Him to send a messenger whose religion is this. And if that were permissible, then it would have been permissible among the former prophets that there was one who was only sent to call to theft, ugly deeds, praising Satan and worshipping him, belittling God, His messenger, and parents, and blaming the good-doer and praising the evil-doer.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage uses a hypothetical scenario to show the absurd consequences of God creating evil acts. If God can create adultery and sodomy, then it would be logically possible for Him to send a prophet whose religion consists of these acts. Extending that, there could have been a prophet who only called to theft, evil deeds, Satan worship, etc. Since such a thing is impossible (God only sends prophets with truth), the premise that God creates evil is false.

Key Terms
الزنا
اللواطة
الرسول
الدعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the eighth proof argue would be permissible if God creates adultery and sodomy?
A) Humans could commit them without punishment.
B) God could send a prophet whose religion is those acts.
C) Satan would be forgiven.
D) Prophets would be unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof demonstrates that if God created evil, it would be logically possible for Him to send a prophet to promote evil, which is impossible, so God does not create evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself blaming your circumstances or even God for creating that inclination? This lesson teaches that God does not create evil—so every evil you commit is your own choice. Will you take full responsibility and repent right now, without shifting blame?
?
The reductio shows that a prophet calling to evil is impossible. Yet, do you follow desires that command you to sin, as if they were a prophet? Which desire in your life has become like a false messenger? What step will you take today to disobey it and obey the true guidance of God?

Torah Evidence for Human Free Will
Torah citation and its three implications for free will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Torah passage about the nearness of the commandment implies human capacity to act and refutes the doctrine of jabr.

Core Idea
The Torah shows that God's command is doable, which requires human freedom.

This passage begins by quoting a key verse from the Torah that emphasizes the accessibility of God's commandments. The author then draws three logical implications from this verse, demonstrating that it necessarily entails human ability to act and choose. Understanding this argument is essential for seeing how scripture supports free will against compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Torah quotation
Read the Arabic passage from 'في التوراة' to 'لتعمل بها' carefully. Notice how it emphasizes that the commandment is near and doable.
This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Identify the three logical implications
After the quotation, the author lists three points (أ, ب, ت). Identify each: (أ) the commandment is the Law, (ب) the purpose is to act, (ت) acting requires ability to do and refrain.
These are marked with Arabic letters.
3
Reflect on the implications for free will
Consider why the third point is crucial: if acting requires ability, then humans must have free choice, contradicting jabr.
This connects the Torah text to the theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التوراة : « إن هذه الوصية التي أوصيك بها اليوم، ليست عسرة عليك . ولا بعيدة منك ليست هي في السماء ، حتى تقول : من يصعد لأجلنا إلى السماء ويأخذها لنا ويسمعنا إياها ، لتعمل بها . ولا هي في عبر الحبر ، حتى تقول : من يعبر لأجلنا البحر ويأخذها لنا ويسمعنا إياها ، لتعمل بها . بل الكلمة قريبة منك جداً . في فمك وفي قلبك لتعمل بها . ومعلوم أن أ - الوصية هي الشريعة . ب . والغرض من الشريعة : العمل بها . ت ـ والعمل بها يستلزم أن يكون الإنسان قادراً على الفعل ، وقادراً على الترك . وفي الإنجيل : يستدل المسيح عليه السلام بنص التوراة السابق على كون الإنسان حراً . يقول المسيح : ( أما كون الإنسان حراً ، فواضح من كتاب موسى . لأن إلهنا عندما أعطى الشريعة على جبل سيناء . قال هكذا : وليست وصيتي في السماء لكي تتخذ لك عذرا قائلاً : من يذهب ليحضر لنا وصية الله ؟ ومن يا ترى يعطينا القوة لنحفظها ؟ ولا هي وراء البحر ، لكي تعد نفسك كما تقدم . بل وصيتي قريبة من قلبك ، حتى إنك تحفظها متى شئت . ... الخ ) . وفي إنجيل لوقا يقول المسيح : ( ولا تبتلوا تقولون في أنفسكم : لنا إبراهيم أبا . لأني أقول لكم : إن الله قادر أن يقيم من هذه الحجارة أولادا لإبراهيم. والآن قد وضعت الفأس على أصل الشجر . فكل شجرة لا تصنع ثمراً جيداً ، تقطع وتلقى في النار ) . وبولس قد حرف نص التوراة عن مواضعه - النص الذي استدل به المسيح على الحرية - بقوله : إن المراد من وصية الله هي الإيمان بالمسيح رباً مصلوباً . وأما العمل فإنه غير واجب على الإنسان إذا آمن بالمسيح ، لأن المسيح ألفى الأعمال بصلبه فداء عن الخطايا . وتفسير بولس باطل لأنه إذا ألغى الأعمال ونادى بأن المسيح هو الإله الخالق للعالم . فإن يكون داعياً إلى هدم الشريعة من أساسها. وفي القرآن الكريم يقول تعالى : ( وما يضل به إلا الفاسقين ) أي من كانت نيته متجهة إلى الضلال واستحب الضلال على الهدى . فإن الله يضله . ولقد سأل المسيح عن سبب خلال الله لفرعون . فأجاب بقوله : ( إنما فعله لأنه نكل بشعبنا وحاول أن يبغى عليه بإبادة كل الأطفال الذكور من إسرائيل ، حتى كاد موسى أن يخسر حياته ) .
In the Torah: 'Indeed this commandment that I command you today is not too hard for you, nor is it far from you. It is not in heaven, that you should say, "Who will ascend to heaven for us and bring it to us and make us hear it, that we may do it?" Neither is it beyond the sea, that you should say, "Who will cross the sea for us and bring it to us and make us hear it, that we may do it?" But the word is very near you. It is in your mouth and in your heart, so that you may do it.' And it is known that A) the commandment is the Law; B) the purpose of the Law is to act by it; C) acting by it requires that man be able to do and able to refrain. In the Gospel, the Messiah (peace be upon him) uses the previous Torah text to prove that man is free. The Messiah says: 'As for man being free, it is clear from the book of Moses. For our God when He gave the Law on Mount Sinai said thus: "My commandment is not in heaven for you to take an excuse saying, 'Who will go to bring us God's commandment? And who will give us the power to keep it?' Nor is it beyond the sea for you to prepare yourself as before. But my commandment is near your heart, so that you keep it whenever you wish.' ... etc.' In the Gospel of Luke, the Messiah says: 'And do not presume to say to yourselves, "We have Abraham as our father." For I tell you, God is able from these stones to raise up children for Abraham. Even now the axe is laid to the root of the trees. Every tree therefore that does not bear good fruit is cut down and thrown into the fire.' Paul distorted the Torah text from its places—the text that the Messiah used to argue for freedom—by saying that the meaning of God's commandment is faith in the Messiah as a crucified lord. As for actions, they are not obligatory for man if he believes in the Messiah, because the Messiah abolished deeds by his crucifixion as atonement for sins. Paul's interpretation is false, because if he nullifies deeds and proclaims that the Messiah is the divine creator of the world, then he becomes a caller to demolishing the Law from its foundation. In the Noble Quran, God Most High says: 'And He misleads not thereby except the defiantly disobedient' i.e., those whose intention is directed towards misguidance and who prefer misguidance over guidance. Then God misleads them. And the Messiah was asked about the reason for God's abandonment of Pharaoh. He answered saying: 'He did that only because he oppressed our people and tried to tyrannize them by killing all the male children of Israel, until Moses nearly lost his life.'
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage provides scriptural evidence from the Torah, Gospels, and Quran to support human free will and refute jabr. The author first quotes Deuteronomy 30:11-14, emphasizing that the commandment is near and doable, which implies human ability to act. He then notes that the Gospel records Jesus using this same passage to argue for human freedom. A citation from Luke 3:8-9 is given, where Jesus warns about bearing good fruit, implying responsibility. The author then criticizes Paul for distorting the Torah text by replacing works with faith alone, arguing that this undermines the Law. Finally, the Quranic verse (2:26) is cited, interpreted to mean that God only leads astray those who already incline towards misguidance. The anecdote about Pharaoh's punishment is presented as a response to the question of why God hardened Pharaoh's heart, attributing it to Pharaoh's own injustice. Thus, all scriptures consistently affirm human capacity and reject the notion of compulsion.

Key Terms
توراة
شريعة
حرية
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the Torah passage necessarily imply?
A) The law is impossible to fulfill
B) Humans must have the ability to act and refrain
C) The law is only for prophets
D) The law is in heaven and requires a mediator

Key Takeaway
The Torah passage and its three logical implications establish that human beings are capable of obeying God's commands, thus rejecting jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God's command is 'not too hard' and 'very near you' – yet how often do you make excuses that obeying a certain rule is impossible or too difficult for you? What specific command do you currently treat as if it were 'in the sky' or 'beyond the sea', and what would it cost you to truly believe it is doable?
?
If you truly internalized that your ability to obey or disobey is real and that God holds you accountable, how would your attitude toward sin change? Which sin have you been rationalizing as 'I couldn't help it,' and does this lesson force you to take full responsibility for it?
?
The Torah passage says the word is 'in your mouth and in your heart so that you may do it' – what is the gap between what you say with your mouth (professing faith) and what you actually do? Will you commit today to close that gap by choosing one act of obedience you have been avoiding?

Gospel Evidence and Refutation of Paul's Distortion
Jesus' use of Torah and critique of Paul's distortion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Jesus used the Torah to argue for human freedom and why Paul's reinterpretation is considered invalid.

Core Idea
Jesus affirmed free will from the Torah; Paul's distortion nullifies the Law.

This part of the passage shows how Jesus himself cites the Torah to prove human freedom. It also includes a warning from the Gospel of Luke about bearing fruit. The author then critiques Paul for misinterpreting the Torah to replace works with faith, which would demolish the Law. These points reinforce the consistent scriptural support for free will.

Guided Path
1
Read Jesus' argument from the Torah
Read the Arabic from 'وفي الإنجيل' to 'مت شئت' . Notice that Jesus uses the same Torah verse to directly argue that man is free.
Jesus' quote slightly paraphrases the Torah.
2
Read the warning from Luke's Gospel
Read the next citation from the Gospel of Luke, where Jesus warns about producing good fruit. This implies responsibility and accountability.
This warning only makes sense if humans have free choice.
3
Understand Paul's distortion and its refutation
Read the author's critique of Paul. Paul replaced the meaning with faith in a crucified Christ, abolishing deeds. The author argues this destroys the Law entirely.
The author sees Paul's interpretation as a corruption of the original message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التوراة : « إن هذه الوصية التي أوصيك بها اليوم، ليست عسرة عليك . ولا بعيدة منك ليست هي في السماء ، حتى تقول : من يصعد لأجلنا إلى السماء ويأخذها لنا ويسمعنا إياها ، لتعمل بها . ولا هي في عبر الحبر ، حتى تقول : من يعبر لأجلنا البحر ويأخذها لنا ويسمعنا إياها ، لتعمل بها . بل الكلمة قريبة منك جداً . في فمك وفي قلبك لتعمل بها . ومعلوم أن أ - الوصية هي الشريعة . ب . والغرض من الشريعة : العمل بها . ت ـ والعمل بها يستلزم أن يكون الإنسان قادراً على الفعل ، وقادراً على الترك . وفي الإنجيل : يستدل المسيح عليه السلام بنص التوراة السابق على كون الإنسان حراً . يقول المسيح : ( أما كون الإنسان حراً ، فواضح من كتاب موسى . لأن إلهنا عندما أعطى الشريعة على جبل سيناء . قال هكذا : وليست وصيتي في السماء لكي تتخذ لك عذرا قائلاً : من يذهب ليحضر لنا وصية الله ؟ ومن يا ترى يعطينا القوة لنحفظها ؟ ولا هي وراء البحر ، لكي تعد نفسك كما تقدم . بل وصيتي قريبة من قلبك ، حتى إنك تحفظها متى شئت . ... الخ ) . وفي إنجيل لوقا يقول المسيح : ( ولا تبتلوا تقولون في أنفسكم : لنا إبراهيم أبا . لأني أقول لكم : إن الله قادر أن يقيم من هذه الحجارة أولادا لإبراهيم. والآن قد وضعت الفأس على أصل الشجر . فكل شجرة لا تصنع ثمراً جيداً ، تقطع وتلقى في النار ) . وبولس قد حرف نص التوراة عن مواضعه - النص الذي استدل به المسيح على الحرية - بقوله : إن المراد من وصية الله هي الإيمان بالمسيح رباً مصلوباً . وأما العمل فإنه غير واجب على الإنسان إذا آمن بالمسيح ، لأن المسيح ألفى الأعمال بصلبه فداء عن الخطايا . وتفسير بولس باطل لأنه إذا ألغى الأعمال ونادى بأن المسيح هو الإله الخالق للعالم . فإن يكون داعياً إلى هدم الشريعة من أساسها. وفي القرآن الكريم يقول تعالى : ( وما يضل به إلا الفاسقين ) أي من كانت نيته متجهة إلى الضلال واستحب الضلال على الهدى . فإن الله يضله . ولقد سأل المسيح عن سبب خلال الله لفرعون . فأجاب بقوله : ( إنما فعله لأنه نكل بشعبنا وحاول أن يبغى عليه بإبادة كل الأطفال الذكور من إسرائيل ، حتى كاد موسى أن يخسر حياته ) .

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Paul's interpretation that faith replaces works is a valid Christian doctrine.
Correction: The author argues that Paul's view is a distortion that demolishes the Law and is therefore false.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is Paul's interpretation of the Torah false?
A) Because it makes the law impossible
B) Because it nullifies deeds and demolishes the Law
C) Because it contradicts the Quran
D) Because it denies Jesus' divinity

Key Takeaway
Jesus' use of the Torah and his warning about fruit bearing confirm free will, and Paul's distortion is rejected because it undermines the Law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Jesus said the commandment is near your heart, so you can keep it whenever you wish. When do you make excuses saying 'who will bring it for me?' or 'who will give me strength?' What is one command from Allah you have been avoiding because you think it is too hard? This week, take one small step to act on it without waiting for a perfect moment.
?
The passage warns against relying on 'We have Abraham as our father.' Are you depending on your family's Islam or your own deeds? What good fruit have you produced lately that shows you are personally accountable? List one deed you will do today solely because you know it is right, not because of who your parents are.
?
Paul's distortion removed the need for deeds. Have you ever acted as if Allah's mercy exempts you from effort, thinking 'I'm a Muslim, so I'm fine'? Where in your life have you replaced obedience with a comfortable excuse? What is one act of worship you have been neglecting that you will recommit to starting tomorrow morning?

Quranic Evidence and the Case of Pharaoh
Quranic verse and anecdote about Pharaoh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse about misguidance and the anecdote about Pharaoh support free will by attributing misguidance to people's own choice.

Core Idea
God only leads astray those who choose misguidance, affirming human responsibility.

The final part of the passage presents a Quranic verse that some might interpret as supporting jabr. The author clarifies that it refers to those who already prefer misguidance. Then an anecdote from Jesus explains Pharaoh's punishment as a result of his own injustice. This shows that scriptural accounts consistently attribute actions to human choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse and its interpretation
Read the Arabic from 'وفي القرآن الكريم' to 'فإن الله يضله'. Note how the author interprets the verse: God only misleads those who already intend misguidance.
The verse is often cited by determinists; this interpretation refutes them.
2
Read the anecdote about Pharaoh
Read from 'ولقد سأل المسيح' to the end. Jesus explains that Pharaoh's hardening was due to his own oppression, not arbitrary divine will.
This anecdote reinforces the idea that punishment is a consequence of personal injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التوراة : « إن هذه الوصية التي أوصيك بها اليوم، ليست عسرة عليك . ولا بعيدة منك ليست هي في السماء ، حتى تقول : من يصعد لأجلنا إلى السماء ويأخذها لنا ويسمعنا إياها ، لتعمل بها . ولا هي في عبر الحبر ، حتى تقول : من يعبر لأجلنا البحر ويأخذها لنا ويسمعنا إياها ، لتعمل بها . بل الكلمة قريبة منك جداً . في فمك وفي قلبك لتعمل بها . ومعلوم أن أ - الوصية هي الشريعة . ب . والغرض من الشريعة : العمل بها . ت ـ والعمل بها يستلزم أن يكون الإنسان قادراً على الفعل ، وقادراً على الترك . وفي الإنجيل : يستدل المسيح عليه السلام بنص التوراة السابق على كون الإنسان حراً . يقول المسيح : ( أما كون الإنسان حراً ، فواضح من كتاب موسى . لأن إلهنا عندما أعطى الشريعة على جبل سيناء . قال هكذا : وليست وصيتي في السماء لكي تتخذ لك عذرا قائلاً : من يذهب ليحضر لنا وصية الله ؟ ومن يا ترى يعطينا القوة لنحفظها ؟ ولا هي وراء البحر ، لكي تعد نفسك كما تقدم . بل وصيتي قريبة من قلبك ، حتى إنك تحفظها متى شئت . ... الخ ) . وفي إنجيل لوقا يقول المسيح : ( ولا تبتلوا تقولون في أنفسكم : لنا إبراهيم أبا . لأني أقول لكم : إن الله قادر أن يقيم من هذه الحجارة أولادا لإبراهيم. والآن قد وضعت الفأس على أصل الشجر . فكل شجرة لا تصنع ثمراً جيداً ، تقطع وتلقى في النار ) . وبولس قد حرف نص التوراة عن مواضعه - النص الذي استدل به المسيح على الحرية - بقوله : إن المراد من وصية الله هي الإيمان بالمسيح رباً مصلوباً . وأما العمل فإنه غير واجب على الإنسان إذا آمن بالمسيح ، لأن المسيح ألفى الأعمال بصلبه فداء عن الخطايا . وتفسير بولس باطل لأنه إذا ألغى الأعمال ونادى بأن المسيح هو الإله الخالق للعالم . فإن يكون داعياً إلى هدم الشريعة من أساسها. وفي القرآن الكريم يقول تعالى : ( وما يضل به إلا الفاسقين ) أي من كانت نيته متجهة إلى الضلال واستحب الضلال على الهدى . فإن الله يضله . ولقد سأل المسيح عن سبب خلال الله لفرعون . فأجاب بقوله : ( إنما فعله لأنه نكل بشعبنا وحاول أن يبغى عليه بإبادة كل الأطفال الذكور من إسرائيل ، حتى كاد موسى أن يخسر حياته ) .

Commentary
This passage provides scriptural evidence from the Torah, Gospels, and Quran to support human free will and refute jabr. The author first quotes Deuteronomy 30:11-14, emphasizing that the commandment is near and doable, which implies human ability to act. He then notes that the Gospel records Jesus using this same passage to argue for human freedom. A citation from Luke 3:8-9 is given, where Jesus warns about bearing good fruit, implying responsibility. The author then criticizes Paul for distorting the Torah text by replacing works with faith alone, arguing that this undermines the Law. Finally, the Quranic verse (2:26) is cited, interpreted to mean that God only leads astray those who already incline towards misguidance. The anecdote about Pharaoh's punishment is presented as a response to the question of why God hardened Pharaoh's heart, attributing it to Pharaoh's own injustice. Thus, all scriptures consistently affirm human capacity and reject the notion of compulsion.

Key Terms
قرآن
فاسقين
فرعون
حرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Quranic verse 'God leads astray' proves that God compels people to misguidance.
Correction: The author clarifies that the verse refers only to those who already choose misguidance; God's leading astray is a consequence of their own intention.

Checkpoint
According to the author, who does God mislead according to the Quranic verse?
A) All people without distinction
B) Only those who already intend misguidance and prefer it
C) Only the unbelievers among the People of the Book
D) Only those who were predestined for hell

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse and Pharaoh story show that misguidance and punishment result from people's own choices, not from divine compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God only misleads those who 'prefer misguidance over guidance', what does that reveal about your own heart when you choose sin over obedience? That preference is already a choice. Which sin are you currently preferring, even subtly, that might be opening the door to spiritual misguidance?
?
The story of Pharaoh shows that oppression and injustice toward others hardens the heart and invites divine abandonment. In your daily interactions—with family, colleagues, or even strangers—where are you acting unjustly, whether by word or deed? How might that be affecting your closeness to Allah right now?
?
If you truly believe that divine misguidance is a direct response to your own choices, not an arbitrary fate, what specific step will you take today to turn your intention back towards guidance? Name one habit you will replace with a Quranic verse or prayer.

The Quranic Challenge as Proof of Human Creative Power
Author quotes tahaddi verse and extracts evidence for human creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic challenge (tahaddi) is used in kalam to argue that humans have the power to originate actions.

Core Idea
If humans had no creative power, the Quranic challenge would be meaningless.

This lesson examines a classic kalam proof for human creative power derived from the Quran's challenge to produce a surah like it. The author argues that the challenge only makes sense if humans have some ability to originate actions. By reading the passage, you will see how the logic unfolds and leads to the conclusion that humans are originators (mujidun).

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
First, read the Quranic verse (2:23-24) and its translation. Notice the challenge: produce a surah like it, or else fear the Fire.
The verse is a direct address to doubters.
2
Understand the logic of the challenge
The author explains that a challenge is only meaningful if the one challenged has some capability. The Arabs had the general ability to produce speech, but they could not produce something like the Quran. This indicates that the Quran has a special quality (hususiyya).
This is the core of the evidence.
3
See the conclusion
If humans had no power to originate (ijad/takwin), the challenge would be pointless. Since the umma agrees the challenge is valid, we conclude that humans are originators (mujidun).
The conclusion is based on consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال تعالى : ( وإن كنتم في ريب مما نزلنا على عبدنا . فأتووا بسورة من مثله ) إلى قوله : ( فإن لم تفعلوا ولن تفعلوا فاتقوا النار التي وقودها الناس والحجارة ) ووجه الاستدلال به : إن التحدي مع من يقدر على شيء من الأفعال . لأنه لما كان قادراً على الفعل في الجملة ، ثم عجز عن فعل له معنى خاص . دل ذلك على أن لذلك الفعل خصوصية. لأنه لو عجز ذلك الفاعل عن الإتيان بمسألة ، ولا يقدر على الفعل أصلا فإن التحدي معه لا يفيد الفائدة المطلوبة . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إذا لم يكن العبد قادراً على الإيجاد والتكوين أصلاً ، لم يحصل التحدي بالقرآن على كونه معجزاً . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً بإجماع الأمة ، علمنا : أن العبد موجد . والجواب : إن هذا الإشكال غير وارد علينا . لانا سلمنا أن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي قد يوجب بعض الأفعال . وإذا لم يؤثرا في أمر من الأمور ، علمنا : أن ذلك المتيسر ممتاز عن ذلك المتعسر لمزيد خاصية ، لأجلها حصل ذلك اليسر .
He Almighty said: 'And if you are in doubt about what We have revealed to Our servant, then produce a surah like it' up to His saying: 'But if you do not – and you will never do – then fear the Fire whose fuel is people and stones.' The aspect of using it as evidence: The challenge (al-tahaddi) is directed to one who is capable of something (shay') of actions. Because when he is capable of action in general (fi al-jumla), and then is incapable of a specific action, that indicates that the action has a particularity (hususiyya). Because if the agent were incapable of producing something (mas'ala) and had no ability to act at all, then the challenge would not yield the desired benefit. If this is established, we say: If the servant is not at all capable of origination (ijad) and creation (takwin), then the challenge with the Qur'an as being a miracle would not occur. And since that is false by consensus of the umma, we know that the servant is an originator (mujid). The answer: Indeed this objection does not apply to us. For we acknowledge that the combination of power (qudra) with the motive (al-da'i) may cause some actions. And if they have no effect on any matter, we know that the easy action is distinguished from the difficult one by an additional special quality (mazid khususiyya), due to which that ease occurred.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument for human creative power based on the Quranic challenge (tahaddi), followed by a response from the determinist perspective. The author begins by quoting verses 23-24 of Surah al-Baqarah: 'If you are in doubt about what We have revealed to Our servant, then produce a surah like it...' He then explains the evidence (wajh al-istidlal): A challenge is only meaningful when addressed to someone who has some ability to act. If the person challenged were completely incapable of any action, the challenge would be pointless. Here, the challenge is to produce something like the Quran. Since the Arabs were generally capable of producing speech, but then were unable to produce anything like the Quran, this indicates that the Quran has a special quality (hususiyya). The logic proceeds: If the servant had absolutely no power of origination (ijad) or creation (takwin), then the challenge regarding the Quran's miraculous nature would not stand. But the umma agrees that the challenge is valid and the Quran is a miracle. Therefore, the servant must have some creative power (al-'abdu mujidun). This is a classic kalam argument against absolute determinism. Then comes the determinist response (al-jawab): The determinist says the objection does not apply to them, because they concede that the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da'i) can cause certain actions. The distinction between easy and hard actions is not due to the agent's independent creative power, but due to a special quality (mazid khususiyya) in the easier action that makes it easier. So the challenge is still valid because the power and motive together may produce some actions but not others, without requiring human origination of the action. This response preserves determinism while acknowledging the apparent human role in action.

Key Terms
التحدي
القدرة
الإيجاد
التكوين
المعجز
الخصوصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tahaddi argument only proves the Quran is a miracle, not that humans have creative power.
Correction: The argument uses the fact that the challenge is directed to humans to infer that humans must have some creative power to be capable of originating actions; otherwise the challenge would be meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to the tahaddi argument presented here, what does the inability of the Arabs to produce a surah like the Quran indicate?
A) The Arabs lacked intelligence.
B) The Quran has a special quality (hususiyya).
C) God directly prevented them.
D) Humans have no creative power.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic challenge implies that humans have some power to originate actions, otherwise the challenge would be pointless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe the Quran is a miracle, but have you ever truly felt the weight of the challenge (التحدي) personally? When you read the Quran, does it press on your soul that you could never produce its like, or has familiarity dulled that awe? This week, read one surah as if you had to defend its divine origin—let the question unsettle you.
?
The argument shows you have some power to originate actions (إيجاد). When you sin, do you honestly acknowledge that you yourself originated that action, not that 'it just happened'? Does that accountability stir a genuine fear of the Fire mentioned in the verse? This week, after each sin, pause and say: 'I am the originator (مُوجِد) of this; I have no one to blame but myself.'
?
If the tahaddi challenge were directed at you today—not just at the Arabs—could you say you have no doubt (ريب) about the Quran's origin? What would it take for you to engage with that challenge, not as a doctrine but as a living demand on your heart? This week, read the verse again and let it question your certainty.
